</tr>
</table>
<p>Nine photographs taken by Hoffman reveal how Hitler rehearsed poses and his hand gestures. He asked Hoffmann to take pictures so that he could see how he looked while speaking. Egon Hanfstaengl, son of Hitler's one-time foreign press officer <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hanfstaengl" title="Ernst Hanfstaengl">Ernst "Putzi" Hanfstaengl</a>, said in a documentary, <i>Fatal Attraction of Hitler</i>: "He had that ability which is needed to make people stop thinking critically and just emote."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Posters">Posters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Posters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plakat_der_ewige_Jude,_1937.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Plakat_der_ewige_Jude%2C_1937.jpg/220px-Plakat_der_ewige_Jude%2C_1937.jpg" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Plakat_der_ewige_Jude%2C_1937.jpg/330px-Plakat_der_ewige_Jude%2C_1937.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Plakat_der_ewige_Jude%2C_1937.jpg 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plakat_der_ewige_Jude,_1937.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"The Eternal Jew", 1937.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Poster" title="Poster">Poster</a> art was a mainstay of the Nazi propaganda effort, aimed both at Germany itself and occupied territories. It had several advantages. The visual effect, being striking, would reach the viewer easily.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Posters were also, unlike other forms of propaganda, difficult to avoid.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Imagery frequently drew on <a href="/wiki/Heroic_realism" title="Heroic realism">heroic realism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-design_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-design-108">[108]</a></sup> Nazi youth and the SS were depicted monumentally, with lighting posed to produce grandeur.<sup id="cite_ref-design_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-design-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Word of the Week</i> wall newspapers, <i>Parole der Woche Wandzeitungen</i>, was a poster campaign put in place by the Ministry for Public Enlightenment and Propaganda of the Third Reich. The first edition was distributed on 16 March 1936. Every week an estimated 125,00 posters were administered to the public from 1936 to 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-109">[109]</a></sup> <i>Word of the Week</i> posters were politically skewed and meant to rally public opinion in support of the Nazi efforts. The posters set out to educate and unify the German people before and especially, during World War II.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG/220px-Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG/330px-Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG/440px-Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1308" data-file-height="1832" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Berlin.Gestapo_Museum_024.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<b>English:</b> "When you see this sign", propaganda poster 1941 of a hate campaign against Jews started by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">Goebbels</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The posters were placed in train cars, buses, platforms, ticket windows – anywhere there was dense traffic flow. Very few individuals, at the time, owned a car, most biked, walked, or used public transportation daily. Exposure to the <i>Word of the</i> Week posters was high in German cites. The messages and Nazi ideologies "stared out at the mass public for a week at a time in tens of thousands of places German pedestrians were likely to pass in the course of a day".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Herf" title="Jeffrey Herf">Jeffery Herf</a>, author of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Enemy:_Nazi_Propaganda_During_World_War_II_and_the_Holocaust" class="mw-redirect" title="The Jewish Enemy: Nazi Propaganda During World War II and the Holocaust">The Jewish Enemy: Nazi Propaganda During World War II and the Holocaust</a></i>, described the poster campaign as a "combination of newspaper editorial, political leaflet, political poster, and tabloid journalism".<sup id="cite_ref-:1_109-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-109">[109]</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> personally appointed artist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Schweitzer" title="Hans Schweitzer">Hans Schweitzer</a>, known as Mjölnir, with the task of translating Nazi ideology into images for the wall newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-110">[110]</a></sup> The posters were 100 centimeters high and 212 centimeters wide.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_109-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-109">[109]</a></sup> The visual style of the posters was bold text and Nazi influenced colors, it meant to capture the attention of the German passersby. The text was big so that several people could read it at the same time and from a distance of a few feet.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_109-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The majority of the posters were centered on Jews and the Allied countries of Great Britain, the United States of America and Russia. During the time period when anti-Semitic articles decreased in publications, the anti-Semitic rhetoric was ramped up in <i>The Word of Week</i> posters. From 1941 to 1943 about twenty-five percent of <i>The Word of</i> Week posters included an attack on Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_109-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-109">[109]</a></sup> The Jews were depicted as enemies because of their supposed economic war, capitalism, and connection to the <a href="/wiki/Bolsheviks" title="Bolsheviks">Bolshevik</a> revolution in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-110">[110]</a></sup> The Nazi regime fostered the idea that the Jews were the masterminds behind all oppositional political force. Images often showed a Jewish figure positioned behind, or above, symbols of economic and political influence.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_110-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-110">[110]</a></sup> Additionally, it was also common to depict the Allied forces of Great Britain, the United States and Russia as overtaken by Jewry.</p>
<p>Posters were also used in schools, depicting, for instance, an institution for the feeble-minded on one hand and houses on the other, to inform the students that the annual cost of this institution would build 17 homes for healthy families.<sup id="cite_ref-schoolcharts_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schoolcharts-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nazi_propaganda_-_Give_me_four_years%E2%80%99_time_-_1937.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c0/Nazi_propaganda_-_Give_me_four_years%E2%80%99_time_-_1937.jpg/220px-Nazi_propaganda_-_Give_me_four_years%E2%80%99_time_-_1937.jpg" width="220" height="384" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/c0/Nazi_propaganda_-_Give_me_four_years%E2%80%99_time_-_1937.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="257" data-file-height="448" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nazi_propaganda_-_Give_me_four_years%E2%80%99_time_-_1937.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Give me four years' time", <a href="/wiki/Photomontage" title="Photomontage">photomontage</a> poster, 1937.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio">Radio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Radio">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before Hitler came to power, he rarely used radio to connect with the public, and when he did so non-party newspapers were allowed to publish his speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-Bytwerk2008_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bytwerk2008-112">[112]</a></sup> This changed soon after he came to power in 1933. Hitler's speeches became widely broadcast all over Germany, especially on the radio, itself introduced by the Ministry of Propaganda. They were shown in weekly newsreels, and reprinted in large editions in books and pamphlets all across Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Bytwerk2008_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bytwerk2008-112">[112]</a></sup> Hitler's speeches became so significant to the Nazis that even restaurants and pubs were expected have their radios on whenever he was delivering one, and in some cities public speakers were used so passersby could hear them.<sup id="cite_ref-Bytwerk2008_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bytwerk2008-112">[112]</a></sup> Nazi propaganda emphasized and portrayed his speeches so that their main points appeared in weekly posters and were all over Germany by the hundreds of thousands.<sup id="cite_ref-Bytwerk2008_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bytwerk2008-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nazi propaganda also used radio as an important tool to promote <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RadioProp_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadioProp-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_broadcasts">Internal broadcasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Internal broadcasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Recognising the importance of radio in disseminating the Nazi message, Goebbels approved a scheme whereby millions of cheap radio sets (the <i><a href="/wiki/Volksempf%C3%A4nger" title="Volksempfänger">Volksempfänger</a></i>) were subsidised by the government. In the "<i>Radio as the Eighth Great Power</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> speech, Goebbels proclaimed:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i><b>It would not have been possible</b> for us to take power or to use it in the ways we have <b>without the radio</b>....It is no exaggeration to say that the German revolution, at least in the form it took, would have been impossible without the airplane and the radio. ...[Radio] reached the entire nation, regardless of class, standing, or religion. That was primarily <b>the result of the tight centralization,</b> the strong reporting, and the up-to-date nature of the German radio....Above all it is necessary to clearly centralize all radio activities, to place spiritual tasks ahead of technical ones,...to provide a clear worldview,</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>By the start of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, over 70% of German households had one of these radios, which were deliberately limited in range in order to prevent loyal citizens from <b>considering other viewpoints</b> in foreign broadcasts.<sup id="cite_ref-RadioProp_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadioProp-113">[113]</a></sup> Radio broadcasts were also played over loudspeakers in public places and workplaces.<sup id="cite_ref-RadioProp_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadioProp-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In private homes, however, people could easily turn off the radio when bored and did so once the novelty of hearing the voice from a box wore off; this caused the Nazis to introduce many non-propaganda elements, such as music, advice and tips, serials and other entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> This was accelerated during the war to prevent people from tuning in enemy propaganda broadcasts; though Goebbels claimed in his <i>Das Reich</i> article that it was to make the radio a good companion to the people, he admitted the truth in his diary.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="External_broadcasts">External broadcasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: External broadcasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_William_Joyce,_Germany,_1945_BU6910.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/The_Capture_of_William_Joyce%2C_Germany%2C_1945_BU6910.jpg/220px-The_Capture_of_William_Joyce%2C_Germany%2C_1945_BU6910.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/The_Capture_of_William_Joyce%2C_Germany%2C_1945_BU6910.jpg/330px-The_Capture_of_William_Joyce%2C_Germany%2C_1945_BU6910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/The_Capture_of_William_Joyce%2C_Germany%2C_1945_BU6910.jpg/440px-The_Capture_of_William_Joyce%2C_Germany%2C_1945_BU6910.jpg 2x" data-file-width="957" data-file-height="1134" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Capture_of_William_Joyce,_Germany,_1945_BU6910.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/William_Joyce" title="William Joyce">William Joyce</a>, who was "Lord Haw-Haw" to British wartime listeners, now under arrest, lies in an ambulance under armed guard before being taken from <a href="/wiki/British_Second_Army#Germany.2C_1945" class="mw-redirect" title="British Second Army">British Second Army</a> Headquarters to a hospital.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg/220px-Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg/330px-Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg 2x" data-file-width="357" data-file-height="474" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philippe_Henriot_1934.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Philippe_Henriot" title="Philippe Henriot">Philippe Henriot</a> in 1934, who later became a <a href="/wiki/Vichy" title="Vichy">Vichy</a> minister and broadcaster for the Nazis.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As well as domestic broadcasts, the Nazi regime used radio to deliver its message to both occupied territories and enemy states. One of the main targets was the United Kingdom, to which <a href="/wiki/William_Joyce" title="William Joyce">William Joyce</a> broadcast regularly, gaining the nickname '<a href="/wiki/Lord_Haw-Haw" title="Lord Haw-Haw">Lord Haw-Haw</a>'. Joyce first appeared on German radio on 6 September 1939 reading the news in English but soon became noted for his often mischievous propaganda broadcasts.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Joyce was executed in 1946 for <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>. Although Joyce was the most notorious, and most regularly heard, of British propagandists, other broadcasters included <a href="/wiki/Norman_Baillie-Stewart" title="Norman Baillie-Stewart">Norman Baillie-Stewart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a>-born teacher <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Vardon" title="Pearl Vardon">Pearl Vardon</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Union_of_Fascists" title="British Union of Fascists">British Union of Fascists</a> members <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Banning" title="Leonard Banning">Leonard Banning</a> and Susan Hilton, Barry Payne Jones of <a href="/wiki/The_Link_(organisation)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Link (organisation)">the Link</a> and Alexander Fraser Grant, whose show was aimed specifically at <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, also broadcasting through the 'New British Broadcasting Service'.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Broadcasts were also made to the United States, notably by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Henry_Best" title="Robert Henry Best">Robert Henry Best</a> and '<a href="/wiki/Axis_Sally" title="Axis Sally">Axis Sally</a>' <a href="/wiki/Mildred_Gillars" title="Mildred Gillars">Mildred Gillars</a>. Best, a freelance journalist based in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, was initially arrested following the German declaration of war on the US but before long he became a feature on propaganda radio, attacking the influence of the Jews in the US and the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" class="mw-redirect" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">Franklin Delano Roosevelt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> who succeeded <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> in Nazi propaganda as "World-Enemy Number One".<sup id="cite_ref-smith1942_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1942-120">[120]</a></sup> Best was later sentenced to life imprisonment for treason. Gillars, a teacher in Germany, mostly broadcast on similar themes as well as peppering her speech with allegations of infidelity against the wives of servicemen. Her most notorious broadcast was the 'Vision of Invasion' radio play, broadcast immediately prior to <a href="/wiki/D-Day" class="mw-redirect" title="D-Day">D-Day</a>, from the perspective of an American mother who dreamed that her soldier son died violently in <a href="/wiki/Normandy" title="Normandy">Normandy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>France also received broadcasts from Radio-Stuttgart, where <a href="/wiki/Paul_Ferdonnet" title="Paul Ferdonnet">Paul Ferdonnet</a>, an anti-Semitic journalist, was the main voice during the <a href="/wiki/Phoney_War" title="Phoney War">Phoney War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Following the occupation, <a href="/wiki/Radio_Paris" title="Radio Paris">Radio Paris</a> and Radio Vichy became the main organs of propaganda, with leading far-right figures such as <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Doriot" title="Jacques Doriot">Jacques Doriot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Henriot" title="Philippe Henriot">Philippe Henriot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_H%C3%A9rold-Paquis" title="Jean Hérold-Paquis">Jean Hérold-Paquis</a> regularly speaking in support of the Nazis. Others who broadcast included Gerald Hewitt, a British citizen who lived most of his life in Paris and had been associated with <a href="/wiki/Action_Fran%C3%A7aise" title="Action Française">Action Française</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Domestic broadcasters were also used to galvanise support for occupation in <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Ward_Hermans" title="Ward Hermans">Ward Hermans</a> regularly spoke in support of the Nazis from his base in <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Little_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little-124">[124]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Republic" title="Italian Social Republic">Italian Social Republic</a>, to where <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Preziosi" title="Giovanni Preziosi">Giovanni Preziosi</a> broadcast a vehemently anti-Semitic show from his base in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Pro-Nazi radio broadcasts in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a> aired in North Africa, crafted with the help of <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Amin_al-Husayni" class="mw-redirect" title="Mohammad Amin al-Husayni">Mohammad Amin al-Husayni</a> and other Arab exiles in Berlin to highlight Arab nationalism. They recast Nazi racist ideology to target Jews alone, not all Semites. Downplaying Mussolini's operations in Africa, they touted the anti-colonialism of the Axis powers.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speakers">Speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Nazi party relied heavily on speakers to make its propaganda presentations, most heavily before they came to power, but also afterwards. Hitler, in <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i>, recounted that he had realized that it was not written matter but the spoken word that brought about changes, as people would not read things that disagreed, but would linger to hear a speaker.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Furthermore, speakers, having their audiences before them, could see their reactions and adjust accordingly, to persuade.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> His own oratory was a major factor in his rise, and he despised those who came to read pre-written speeches.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Such speakers were particularly important when it was not wanted that the information put across not reach foreigners, who could access the mass media.<sup id="cite_ref-twilight_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twilight-131">[131]</a></sup> Schools were instituted to substitute for the political conflict that had formed the old speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> In 1939, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Walter_Tiessler&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Walter Tiessler (page does not exist)">Walter Tiessler</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Tie%C3%9Fler" class="extiw" title="de:Walter Tießler">de</a>)</span>, speaking of his own experience as an early speaker, urged that they continue.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">Sturmabteilung</a> speakers were used, though their reliance on instinct sometimes offended well-educated audiences, but their blunt and folksy manner often had their own appeal.<sup id="cite_ref-Claudia_Koonz_p_89_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claudia_Koonz_p_89-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ministry would provide such speakers with information, such as how to spin the problems on the eastern front,<sup id="cite_ref-frostbite_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frostbite-135">[135]</a></sup> or how to discuss the cuts in food rations.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> The party propaganda headquarters, sent the <i>Redner-Schnellinformation</i> [Speakers' Express Information] out with guidelines for immediate campaigns, such as anti-Semitic campaigns and what information to present.<sup id="cite_ref-twilight_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twilight-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Specific groups were targeted with such speakers. Speakers, for instance, were created specifically for Hitler Youth.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> These would, among other things, lecture <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/League_of_German_Girls" title="League of German Girls">BDM</a> on the need to produce more children.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Speakers often addressed political or military rallies, which were well-orchestrated events with banners and marching bands.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes">Themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Themes_in_Nazi_propaganda" title="Themes in Nazi propaganda">Themes in Nazi propaganda</a></div>
<p>Nazi propaganda promoted <a href="/wiki/Nazi_ideology" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi ideology">Nazi ideology</a> by demonizing the enemies of the Nazi Party, especially <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> and communists, but also capitalists and intellectuals. The campaign against Jews was especially intensive, and was a direct result of <a href="/wiki/Hitler" class="mw-redirect" title="Hitler">Hitler</a>'s influence on Nazi policy.</p>
<p>Propaganda also promoted the values asserted by the Nazis, including heroic death, <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrerprinzip" title="Führerprinzip">Führerprinzip</a></i> (leader principle), <i><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i> (people's community), <i><a href="/wiki/Blut_und_Boden" class="mw-redirect" title="Blut und Boden">Blut und Boden</a></i> (blood and soil) and the youth were taught early to take pride in the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Master_race" title="Master race">master race</a> (<i>Herrenvolk</i>). Propaganda was also used to maintain the <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">cult of personality</a> around Nazi leader <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, and to promote campaigns for <a href="/wiki/Nazi_eugenics" title="Nazi eugenics">eugenics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Heim_ins_Reich" title="Heim ins Reich">annexation of German-speaking areas</a>.</p>
<p>After the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, Nazi propaganda vilified Germany's enemies, notably <a href="/wiki/Nazi_propaganda_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nazi propaganda and the United Kingdom">the United Kingdom</a>, the Soviet Union and the United States, and exhorted the population to partake in <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propaganda_in_Nazi_Germany&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nazi propaganda is a relatively recent topic of close study.<sup id="cite_ref-Welch_4_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welch_4-140">[140]</a></sup> Historians of all persuasions, including <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> writers, agree about its remarkable effectiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-Welch_4_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welch_4-140">[140]</a></sup> Their assessment of its significance, however – whether it <i>shaped</i> or merely directed and exploited public opinion – is influenced by their approach to wider questions raised by the study of Nazi Germany, such as the question whether the Nazi state was a fully <a href="/wiki/Totalitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> dictatorship, as argued by <a href="/wiki/Hannah_Arendt" title="Hannah Arendt">Hannah Arendt</a>, or whether it also depended on a certain societal consensus.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to media archives, an important primary source for the study of the Nazi propaganda effort are the reports on civilian morale and public opinion that the <i><a href="/wiki/Sicherheitsdienst" title="Sicherheitsdienst">Sicherheitsdienst</a></i> and later the RMVP compiled from 1939 on. Another are the <i>Deutschland-Berichte</i>, reports gathered by underground agents of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sopade" title="Sopade">Sopade</a></i> that particularly dealt with German popular opinion.<sup id="cite_ref-Welch_7_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welch_7-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
